For a guide to IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Cornish</b> (<i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Kernowek</i></i>) is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_revived_languages" title="List of revived languages">revived language</a> that became extinct as a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a> in the late 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Brittonic_languages" title="Southwestern Brittonic languages">Southwestern</a> <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Celtic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic language">Celtic language</a> that is native to <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> in south-west England. <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language_revival" title="Cornish language revival">A revival</a> began in the early 20th century. Some have expressed the opinion that the language is an important part of Cornish identity, culture and heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Cornish is currently a recognised <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">minority language</a> under the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It has a growing number of <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-Diarmuid_O&#39;Neill_240_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diarmuid_O&#39;Neill_240-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> A few parents are inspired to create new <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a> speakers, by teaching their children the language from birth.<sup id="cite_ref-Linguistic_minorities_in_countries_belonging_to_the_European_community_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Linguistic_minorities_in_countries_belonging_to_the_European_community-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mebyon_Kernow_and_Cornish_Nationalism_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mebyon_Kernow_and_Cornish_Nationalism-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scholarship_and_the_Gypsy_Struggle_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scholarship_and_the_Gypsy_Struggle-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ball_&amp;_Muller:_The_Celtic_Languages_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball_&amp;_Muller:_The_Celtic_Languages-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Along with <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a>, Cornish is descended directly from the <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Common Brittonic</a> language spoken throughout much of Britain before the English language came to dominate. It was the main language of Cornwall for centuries until it was pushed westwards by English, maintaining close links with its sister language <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a>, with which it was <a href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a> until well into the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. Cornish continued to function as a <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">common community language</a> in parts of <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> until the late 18th century and continued to be spoken in the home by some families into the 19th and possibly 20th centuries, overlapping the beginning of revival efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> A process to revive the language was begun in the early 20th century, with a number of orthographical systems still in use, although an attempt was made to impose a <a href="/wiki/Standard_Written_Form" title="Standard Written Form">Standard Written Form</a> in 2008. In 2010, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> announced that its former classification of the language as "extinct" was "no longer accurate".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the revival of the language, some Cornish textbooks and <a href="/wiki/Cornish_literature" title="Cornish literature">works of literature</a> have been published, and an increasing number of people are studying the language.<sup id="cite_ref-Diarmuid_O&#39;Neill_240_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diarmuid_O&#39;Neill_240-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Recent developments include <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Cornwall" title="Music of Cornwall">Cornish music</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Independent_film" title="Independent film">independent films</a><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and children\'s books. A small number of people in Cornwall have been brought up to be <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">bilingual</a> native speakers,<sup id="cite_ref-magakernow797_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-magakernow797-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-e18_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e18-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and the language is taught in many schools.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The first Cornish language <a href="/wiki/Day_care" class="mw-redirect" title="Day care">cr\xc3\xa8che</a> opened in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Classification"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Classification</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Cultural_events"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Cultural events</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Study_and_teaching"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Study and teaching</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Cornish_studies"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Cornish studies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-19"><a href="#Neo-Cornish_literature"><span class="tocnumber">7.4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Neo-Cornish literature</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Media"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">Media</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Music"><span class="toctext">Samples</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Dictionaries"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Dictionaries</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Cornish is one of the <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic languages</a>, which constitute a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">Insular Celtic</a> section of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic language family</a>. Brittonic also includes <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumbric language">Cumbric language</a>; the last is <a href="/wiki/Extinct_language" title="Extinct language">extinct</a>. <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a> are part of the separate <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic</a> branch of Insular Celtic.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Loth" title="Joseph Loth">Joseph Loth</a> viewed Cornish and Breton as being two dialects of the same language, claiming that "Middle Cornish is without doubt closer to Breton as a whole than the modern Breton dialect of <a href="/wiki/Quiberon" title="Quiberon">Quiberon</a> [<i>Kiberen</i>] is to that of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Pol-de-L%C3%A9on" title="Saint-Pol-de-L\xc3\xa9on">Saint-Pol-de-L\xc3\xa9on</a> [<i>Kastell-Paol</i>]."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Britonia6hcentury.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Britonia6hcentury.png/170px-Britonia6hcentury.png" width="170" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Britonia6hcentury.png/255px-Britonia6hcentury.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Britonia6hcentury.png/340px-Britonia6hcentury.png 2x" data-file-width="1670" data-file-height="2928" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Britonia6hcentury.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (Celtic people)">Brittonic speakers</a> around the 6th century. Cornish and <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a> are very closely related</div></div></div>\n<p>Cornish evolved from the <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Common Brittonic</a> spoken throughout Britain south of the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a> during the <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">British Iron Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman period</a>. As a result of <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">westward Anglo-Saxon expansion</a>, the Britons of the southwest were separated from those in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>. Some scholars have proposed that this split took place after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Deorham" title="Battle of Deorham">Battle of Deorham</a> in about 577. The western dialects eventually evolved into <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">modern Welsh</a> and the now extinct <a href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumbric language">Cumbric</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Brittonic_languages" title="Southwestern Brittonic languages">Southwestern Brittonic</a> developed into Cornish and Breton, the latter as a result of emigration to parts of the continent, known as <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> over the following centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Cornish">Old Cornish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Old Cornish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The area controlled by the southwestern Britons was progressively reduced by the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a> over the next few centuries. During the Old Cornish period (800\xe2\x80\x931200), the Cornish-speaking area was largely coterminous with modern-day <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>; the region of <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> was isolated by Wessex in 936 AD and many inhabitants fled to Cornwall or <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The earliest written record of the Cornish language comes from this period; a 9th-century <a href="/wiki/Gloss_(annotation)" title="Gloss (annotation)">gloss</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscript</a> of <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Consolation_of_Philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Consolation of Philosophy">De Consolatione Philosophiae</a></i></i> by <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a>, which used the words <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">ud rocashaas</i></i>.  The phrase means "it [the mind] hated the gloomy places".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> A much more substantial survival from Old Cornish is a Cornish-Latin glossary (the <em lang="la">Vocabularium Cornicum</em> or Cottonian Vocabulary) containing translations of around 300 words.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The manuscript was widely thought to be in <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a> until the 1700s when it was identified as Cornish. At this time there was still little difference between Welsh and Cornish, and even fewer differences between Cornish and Breton, with some scholars arguing that the terms "Old Cornish" and "Old Breton" are merely geographical terms for the same language.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Cornish">Middle Cornish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle Cornish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Origo_Mundi_kynsa_gwersow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Origo_Mundi_kynsa_gwersow.jpg/220px-Origo_Mundi_kynsa_gwersow.jpg" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Origo_Mundi_kynsa_gwersow.jpg/330px-Origo_Mundi_kynsa_gwersow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Origo_Mundi_kynsa_gwersow.jpg/440px-Origo_Mundi_kynsa_gwersow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="901" data-file-height="510" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Origo_Mundi_kynsa_gwersow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The opening verses of <i>Origo Mundi</i>, the first play of the <a href="/wiki/Ordinalia" title="Ordinalia">Ordinalia</a> (the <i><a href="/wiki/Masterpiece" title="Masterpiece">magnum opus</a></i> of medieval Cornish literature), written by an unknown monk in the late 14th century</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beunans_Meriasek_(The_life_of_St_Meriasek)_(f.56v.)_Middle_Cornish_Saint%27s_Play.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Beunans_Meriasek_%28The_life_of_St_Meriasek%29_%28f.56v.%29_Middle_Cornish_Saint%27s_Play.jpg/220px-Beunans_Meriasek_%28The_life_of_St_Meriasek%29_%28f.56v.%29_Middle_Cornish_Saint%27s_Play.jpg" width="220" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Beunans_Meriasek_%28The_life_of_St_Meriasek%29_%28f.56v.%29_Middle_Cornish_Saint%27s_Play.jpg/330px-Beunans_Meriasek_%28The_life_of_St_Meriasek%29_%28f.56v.%29_Middle_Cornish_Saint%27s_Play.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Beunans_Meriasek_%28The_life_of_St_Meriasek%29_%28f.56v.%29_Middle_Cornish_Saint%27s_Play.jpg/440px-Beunans_Meriasek_%28The_life_of_St_Meriasek%29_%28f.56v.%29_Middle_Cornish_Saint%27s_Play.jpg 2x" data-file-width="874" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beunans_Meriasek_(The_life_of_St_Meriasek)_(f.56v.)_Middle_Cornish_Saint%27s_Play.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Beunans_Meriasek" title="Beunans Meriasek">Beunans Meriasek</a></i> (The life of St. <a href="/wiki/Meriasek" title="Meriasek">Meriasek</a>) (f.56v.) Middle Cornish Saint\'s Play</div></div></div>\n<p>The Cornish language continued to flourish well through the Middle Cornish period (1200\xe2\x80\x931600), reaching a peak of about 39,000 speakers in the 13th century, after which the number started to decline.<sup id="cite_ref-ken_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ken-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stalmaszczyk_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stalmaszczyk-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This period provided the bulk of traditional Cornish literature, which was used to reconstruct the language during its revival. Most important is the <i><a href="/wiki/Ordinalia" title="Ordinalia">Ordinalia</a></i>, a cycle of three mystery plays, <i>Origo Mundi</i>, <i>Passio Christi</i> and <i>Resurrexio Domini</i>.  Together these provide about 20,000 lines of text. Various plays were written by the canons of <a href="/wiki/Glasney_College" title="Glasney College">Glasney College</a>, intended to educate the Cornish people about the Bible and the Celtic saints. From this period also is <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Beunans_Meriasek" title="Beunans Meriasek">Beunans Meriasek</a></i></i> and the recently discovered <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Bewnans_Ke" title="Bewnans Ke">Bewnans Ke</a></i></i>.\n</p><p>In the reign of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Report" title="Report">account</a> was given by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Boorde" title="Andrew Boorde">Andrew Boorde</a> in his 1542 <i>Boke of the Introduction of Knowledge</i>. He states, "<i lang="enm" title="Middle English language text">In Cornwall is two speches, the one is naughty Englysshe, and the other is Cornysshe speche. And there be many men and women the which cannot speake one worde of Englysshe, but all Cornyshe.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1549" title="Act of Uniformity 1549">Act of Uniformity 1549</a>, people in many areas of Cornwall did not speak or understand English. The intention of the Act was to replace worship in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> with worship in English, which was known by the lawmakers not to be universally spoken throughout England. Instead of merely banning Latin, the Act was framed so as to enforce English. The <a href="/wiki/Prayer_Book_Rebellion" title="Prayer Book Rebellion">Prayer Book Rebellion</a>, which may also have been influenced by the retaliation of the English after the failed <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Rebellion_of_1497" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish Rebellion of 1497">Cornish Rebellion of 1497</a>, broke out, and was ruthlessly suppressed: over 4,000 people who protested against the imposition of an English prayer book were massacred by <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI\'s</a> army. Their leaders were executed and the people suffered numerous reprisals.\n</p><p>The rebels\' document claimed they wanted a return to the old religious services and ended, "We the Cornishmen (whereof certain of us understand no English) utterly refuse this new English [altered spelling]." <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour</a>, Duke of <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a>, replied to the Cornishmen, inquiring as to why they should be offended by services in English when they had them in Latin, which they also did not understand.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_language_shift.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cornish_language_shift.svg/240px-Cornish_language_shift.svg.png" width="240" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cornish_language_shift.svg/360px-Cornish_language_shift.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cornish_language_shift.svg/480px-Cornish_language_shift.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1205" data-file-height="970" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornish_language_shift.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map showing the westward decline of Cornish, 1300\xe2\x80\x931750</div></div></div>\n<p>Through many factors, including loss of life and the spread of English, the <a href="/wiki/Prayer_Book_Rebellion" title="Prayer Book Rebellion">Prayer Book Rebellion</a> proved a turning-point for the Cornish language.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Peter Berresford-Ellis cites the years 1550\xe2\x80\x931650 as a century of immense damage for the language, and its decline can be traced to this period. In 1680, <a href="/wiki/William_Scawen" title="William Scawen">William Scawen</a> wrote an essay describing 16 reasons for the decline of Cornish, among them the lack of a distinctive Cornish alphabet, the loss of contact between Cornwall and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, the cessation of the miracle plays, loss of records in the Civil War, lack of a Cornish bible, and immigration to Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-Berresford-Ellis_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berresford-Ellis-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Modern_Cornish">Early Modern Cornish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early Modern Cornish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lether_Bodinar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Lether_Bodinar.jpg/170px-Lether_Bodinar.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Lether_Bodinar.jpg/255px-Lether_Bodinar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Lether_Bodinar.jpg/340px-Lether_Bodinar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="454" data-file-height="604" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lether_Bodinar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>William Bodinar\'s letter, dated 3 July 1776</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dorothy_Pentreath.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A black and white engraving of a woman in 18th century clothing with a bonnet. Fish, a crab, a crustacean and a jug are below" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Dorothy_Pentreath.jpg/250px-Dorothy_Pentreath.jpg" width="250" height="436" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bc/Dorothy_Pentreath.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="275" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dorothy_Pentreath.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dolly_Pentreath" title="Dolly Pentreath">Dolly Pentreath</a> (died 1777), said to be the last native speaker of Cornish, in an engraved portrait published in 1781</div></div></div><p>\nBy the middle of the 17th century, the language had retreated to <a href="/wiki/Penwith" title="Penwith">Penwith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerrier" title="Kerrier">Kerrier</a>, and transmission of the language to new generations had almost entirely ceased. In his <i>Survey of Cornwall</i>, published in 1602, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Carew_(antiquary)" title="Richard Carew (antiquary)">Richard Carew</a> writes:</p><blockquote><p>[M]ost of the inhabitants can speak no word of Cornish, but very few are ignorant of the English; and yet some so affect their own, as to a stranger they will not speak it; for if meeting them by chance, you inquire the way, or any such matter, your answer shall be, "<i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Meea navidna caw zasawzneck</i>," "I [will] speak no Saxonage."<sup id="cite_ref-Carew1811_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carew1811-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The Late Cornish period from 1578 to about 1800 has fewer sources of information on the language but they are more varied in nature.  Written sources from this period are often spelled following English spelling conventions since the majority of writers of the time had had no exposure to Middle Cornish texts or the Cornish orthography within them, although after 1700 some writers began to adopt the orthography used by Edward Lhuyd in his Archaeologia Brittanica, for example using the circumflex to denote long vowels.  In 1776, <a href="/w/index.php?title=William_Bodinar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="William Bodinar (page does not exist)">William Bodinar</a>, who had learnt Cornish from fishermen, wrote a letter in Cornish which was probably the last prose in the language. However, the last verse was the <i><a href="/wiki/Cranken_Rhyme" title="Cranken Rhyme">Cranken Rhyme</a></i>, written in the late 19th century by <a href="/wiki/John_Davey_(Cornish_speaker)" title="John Davey (Cornish speaker)">John Davey</a> of <a href="/wiki/Boswednack" title="Boswednack">Boswednack</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Last_speaker_of_the_Cornish_language" title="Last speaker of the Cornish language">last native speakers of Cornish</a> are thought to have died by the end of the 18th century.\n</p><p>In the 18th and 19th centuries, there was academic interest in the language and in attempting to find the <a href="/wiki/Last_speaker_of_Cornish" class="mw-redirect" title="Last speaker of Cornish">last speaker of Cornish</a>. This academic interest, along with the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Revival" title="Celtic Revival">Celtic Revival</a> in the late 19th century, provided the groundwork for a Cornish language revival movement.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Decline_of_Cornish_speakers_1200_to_1800.5B29.5D.5B30.5D"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_of_Cornish_speakers_1200_to_1800[29][30]">Decline of Cornish speakers 1200 to 1800<sup id="cite_ref-ken_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ken-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stalmaszczyk_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stalmaszczyk-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Decline of Cornish speakers 1200 to 1800[29][30]">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable" border="1">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Year\n</th>\n<th>Area where Cornish was spoken (in km\xc2\xb2)\n</th>\n<th>Total population of Cornwall\n</th>\n<th>Number of Cornish speakers\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1050\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>16,000\n</td>\n<td>15,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1110\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>21,000\n</td>\n<td>20,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1150\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>28,000\n</td>\n<td>26,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1200\n</td>\n<td>3,270\n</td>\n<td>35,000\n</td>\n<td>30,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1250\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>43,000\n</td>\n<td>34,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1300\n</td>\n<td>2,780\n</td>\n<td>52,000\n</td>\n<td>38,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1350\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>48,000\n</td>\n<td>32,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1400\n</td>\n<td>2,360\n</td>\n<td>55,000\n</td>\n<td>34,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1450\n</td>\n<td>2,360\n</td>\n<td>62,000\n</td>\n<td>33,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1500\n</td>\n<td>1,890\n</td>\n<td>69,000\n</td>\n<td>33,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1550\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>76,000\n</td>\n<td>30,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1600\n</td>\n<td>1,400\n</td>\n<td>84,000\n</td>\n<td>22,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1650\n</td>\n<td>910\n</td>\n<td>93,000\n</td>\n<td>14,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1700\n</td>\n<td>530\n</td>\n<td>106,000\n</td>\n<td>5,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1750\n</td>\n<td>160\n</td>\n<td>140,000\n</td>\n<td>very few\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1800\n</td>\n<td>0\n</td>\n<td>192,000\n</td>\n<td>0\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_Modern_Cornish">Recent Modern Cornish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Recent Modern Cornish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language_revival" title="Cornish language revival">Cornish language revival</a></div>\n<p>In 1904, the Celtic language scholar and Cornish cultural activist <a href="/wiki/Henry_Jenner" title="Henry Jenner">Henry Jenner</a> published <i>A Handbook of the Cornish Language</i>. The publication of this book is often considered to be the point at which the revival movement started.\n</p><p>The revival focused on reconstructing and standardising the language, including coining new words for modern concepts, and creating educational material in order to teach Cornish to others. In 1929 <a href="/wiki/Robert_Morton_Nance" title="Robert Morton Nance">Robert Morton Nance</a> published his <a href="/wiki/Unified_Cornish" title="Unified Cornish">Unified Cornish</a> system, based on the Middle Cornish literature while extending the attested vocabulary with forms based on Celtic roots also found in Breton and Welsh, publishing a dictionary in 1938. Nance\'s work became the basis of revived Cornish for most of the 20th century. However, as the revival grew in strength and focus shifted from written to spoken Cornish, Nance\'s stiff, archaic formulation of the language seemed less suitable for a spoken revival, and academic research into the traditional literature proved that the Unified system lacked some phonological distinctions.\n</p><p>In the 1980s, in response to dissatisfaction with Unified Cornish, <a href="/wiki/Ken_George" title="Ken George">Ken George</a> published a new system, <i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Kernewek_Kemmyn" title="Kernewek Kemmyn">Kernewek Kemmyn</a></i> ("Common Cornish"). Like Unified Cornish, it retained a Middle Cornish base but implemented an orthography that aspired to be as <a href="/wiki/Phonemic_orthography" title="Phonemic orthography">phonemic</a> as possible. It was subsequently adopted by the Cornish Language Board as well as by many Cornish speakers, but came under fierce criticism by academic linguists for its phonological base, as well as those who found its orthography too different from traditional Cornish spelling conventions. Also during this period, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gendall" title="Richard Gendall">Richard Gendall</a> created his <a href="/wiki/Modern_Cornish" title="Modern Cornish">Modern Cornish</a> system (also known as "Revived Late Cornish"), which used Late Cornish as a basis, and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Williams" title="Nicholas Williams">Nicholas Williams</a> published a revised version of Unified; however neither of these systems gained the popularity of Unified or Kemmyn.\n</p><p>The revival entered a period of factionalism and public disputes, with each orthography attempting to push the others aside. By the time that Cornish was recognised by the UK government under the European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages in 2002, it had become recognised that the existence of multiple orthographies was unsustainable with regards to using the language in education and public life, as none had achieved a wide consensus. A process of unification was set about which resulted in the creation of the public-body <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Language_Partnership" title="Cornish Language Partnership">Cornish Language Partnership</a> in 2005 and agreement on a <a href="/wiki/Standard_Written_Form" title="Standard Written Form">Standard Written Form</a> in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In 2010 UNESCO altered its classification of Cornish, stating that its previous label of "extinct" was no longer accurate.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This was seen by Cornish speakers as a milestone, turning the language from a state of undergoing revival, to having been revived.\n</p><p>The modern-day Cornish language is a successfully revived language with a number of speakers that is slowly increasing, and is becoming more visible in Cornwall as local government and business are encouraged to make use of the language as part of revitalisation efforts.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution_and_number_of_speakers">Geographic distribution and number of speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution and number of speakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Szyld_kornwalijski_w_Penzance.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Szyld_kornwalijski_w_Penzance.jpg/220px-Szyld_kornwalijski_w_Penzance.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Szyld_kornwalijski_w_Penzance.jpg/330px-Szyld_kornwalijski_w_Penzance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Szyld_kornwalijski_w_Penzance.jpg/440px-Szyld_kornwalijski_w_Penzance.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="479" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Szyld_kornwalijski_w_Penzance.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cornish can be seen in many places in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Speakers of Cornish reside primarily in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, which has a population of 563,600 (2017 estimate). There are also some speakers living outside Cornwall, particularly in the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_diaspora" title="Cornish diaspora">Cornish diaspora</a>, as well as other <a href="/wiki/Celtic_nations" title="Celtic nations">Celtic nations</a>. Estimates of the number of Cornish speakers vary according to the definition of being a speaker, and is difficult to accurately determine due to the individualised nature of language take-up. Nevertheless, there is recognition that the number of Cornish speakers is growing.<sup id="cite_ref-Diarmuid_O&#39;Neill_240_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diarmuid_O&#39;Neill_240-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> From before the 1980s to the end of the 20th century there was a sixfold increased in the number of speakers to around 300.<sup id="cite_ref-ekeltoi1_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ekeltoi1-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> One figure for the mean number of people who know a few basic words, such as knowing that "Kernow" means "Cornwall", was 300,000; the same survey gave the figure of people able to have simple conversations at 3,000.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The Cornish Language Strategy project commissioned research to provide quantitative and qualitative evidence for the number of Cornish speakers: due to the success of the revival project it was estimated that 2,000 people were fluent (surveyed in spring 2008), an increase from the estimated 300 people who spoke Cornish fluently suggested in a study by Kenneth MacKinnon in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_BBC/British_Council_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_BBC/British_Council-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Walesonline_creche_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walesonline_creche-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jenefer Lowe of the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Language_Partnership" title="Cornish Language Partnership">Cornish Language Partnership</a> said in an interview with the BBC in 2010 that there were around 300 fluent speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornwall Council</a> estimated in 2015 that there were 300\xe2\x80\x93400 fluent speakers who used the language regularly, with 5,000 people having a basic conversational ability in the language.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A report on the 2011 Census published in 2013 by the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics</a> placed the number of speakers at somewhere from 325 to 625 speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In 2017 the ONS released a <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_information" title="Freedom of information">freedom of information</a> request based on the 2011 Census which placed the number of speakers at 557 people in England and Wales declared Cornish to be their main language, 464 of whom lived in Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Institute of Cornish Studies at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Exeter" title="University of Exeter">University of Exeter</a> is working with the Cornish Language Partnership to study the Cornish language revival of the 20th Century, including the growth in number of speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognised_minority_language_status">Recognised minority language status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Recognised minority language status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_south_from_Carn_Brea_beacon,_West_Penwith_-_geograph.org.uk_-_51844.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/View_south_from_Carn_Brea_beacon%2C_West_Penwith_-_geograph.org.uk_-_51844.jpg/220px-View_south_from_Carn_Brea_beacon%2C_West_Penwith_-_geograph.org.uk_-_51844.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/View_south_from_Carn_Brea_beacon%2C_West_Penwith_-_geograph.org.uk_-_51844.jpg/330px-View_south_from_Carn_Brea_beacon%2C_West_Penwith_-_geograph.org.uk_-_51844.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/View_south_from_Carn_Brea_beacon%2C_West_Penwith_-_geograph.org.uk_-_51844.jpg/440px-View_south_from_Carn_Brea_beacon%2C_West_Penwith_-_geograph.org.uk_-_51844.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="486" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_south_from_Carn_Brea_beacon,_West_Penwith_-_geograph.org.uk_-_51844.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The view from Carn Brea beacon (<i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Karn Bre</i></i>) in <a href="/wiki/Penwith" title="Penwith">Penwith</a> (<i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Pennwydh</i></i>), near <a href="/wiki/Crows-an-Wra" title="Crows-an-Wra">Crows-an-Wra</a> (<i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Krows an Wragh</i></i>), looking towards the village of Treave (<i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Trev</i></i>) with <a href="/wiki/Porthcurno" title="Porthcurno">Porthcurno</a> (<i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Porthkornow</i></i>) in the distance. The Cornish language has had substantial influence on Cornwall\'s toponomy and nomenclature.</div></div></div>\n<p>Cornish has no official status anywhere but, since 2002, it has been recognised as a minority language under the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Language_Partnership" title="Cornish Language Partnership">Cornish Language Partnership</a> promotes and develops the language in Cornwall.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornwall Council</a>\'s policy is to support the language, in line with the European Charter. A motion was passed in November 2009 in which the council promoted the inclusion of Cornish, as appropriate and where possible, in council publications and on signs.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> This plan has drawn some criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2015, <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_Council" title="Cornwall Council">Cornwall Council</a> announced that staff would be encouraged to use "basic words and phrases" in Cornish when dealing with the public.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\nIn 2014 the Cornish people were recognised by the UK Government as a national minority under the <a href="/wiki/Framework_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_National_Minorities" title="Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities">Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The FCNM provides certain rights and protections to a national minority with regard to their minority language.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>\'s <i>Atlas of World Languages</i> classifies Cornish as "critically endangered". UNESCO has said that a previous classification of "extinct", which came under fierce criticism from Cornish speakers, "does not reflect the current situation for Cornish".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, British government funding for the Cornish language ceased, and responsibility transferred to Cornwall council.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA"><img alt="[]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/30px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png" width="30" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/45px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/60px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="75" data-file-height="100" /></a></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>&#32;instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The phonology of modern Cornish is based on a number of sources. The work of the linguist <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lhuyd" title="Edward Lhuyd">Edward Lhuyd</a> who visited Cornwall in 1700 to record the language, as well as the modern <a href="/wiki/Cornish_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornish dialect">Cornish dialect</a> and accent of English, which got much of its intonation and sounds from the Cornish language,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> have provided a major source of input. Analysis of the traditional literature has also been used, as the Middle Cornish plays were often written in rhyming verse, and Late Cornish texts were written phonetically following English spelling conventions.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2">\n</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Labial_consonant" title="Labial consonant">Labial</a>\n</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Interdental_consonant" consonant">Palatal</a>\n</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">Velar</a>\n</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Glottal_consonant" title="Glottal (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_affricate" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced palato-alveolar affricate">\xca\xa4</a></span>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the
