Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Open_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open back rounded vowel">\xc9\x92\xcb\x90</a></span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_grammar" title="Cornish grammar">Cornish grammar</a></div>\n<p>The grammar of Cornish shares with other Celtic languages a number of features which, while not unique, are unusual in an Indo-European context. The grammatical features most unfamiliar to English speakers of the language are the initial consonant mutations, the <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93subject%E2%80%93object" title="Verb\xe2\x80\x93subject\xe2\x80\x93object">verb\xe2\x80\x93subject\xe2\x80\x93object</a> word order, <a href="/wiki/Inflected_preposition" title="Inflected preposition">inflected prepositions</a>, fronting of emphasised syntactic elements, and the use of two different forms for "to be". Cornish nouns belong to one of two <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical genders</a>, masculine and feminine, but are not inflected for <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case</a>. Cornish has a variety of different endings to indicate the plural, and some nouns have a third collective form. Verbs are <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">conjugated</a> for <a href="/wiki/Tense-aspect-mood" class="mw-redirect" title="Tense-aspect-mood">tense and mood</a>, which can be indicated either by inflection of the main verb, or by the use of auxiliary verbs. In Cornish vocabulary, a large number of the lexical items are language and culture specific. Examples of these include the Cornish word <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">atal</i></i>, which means "mine waste" and the word <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">beetia</i></i>, which means "to mend fishing nets". <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Foogan</i></i> and <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">hogan</i></i> are different types of pastry cakes. <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Troyl</i></i> is culture specific when referring to "a traditional Cornish dance get-together", while <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Furry</i></i> is a specific kind of ceremonial dance that takes place in Cornwall.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In contrast, Cornish translates the English noun, "book", as <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lyver" class="extiw" title="wikt:lyver">lyver</a></i></i> (cognate with <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> <i><i lang="cy" title="Welsh language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/llyfr" class="extiw" title="wikt:llyfr">llyfr</a></i></i>, but <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">lyver</i></i> can actually be translated into English as "book" or "volume" because it can be considered one in a set of books.\n</p><p>As in other Celtic languages, Cornish lacks a number of verbs that are commonly found in other languages. This includes modals and psych-verbs;<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> examples "have", "like", "hate", "prefer", "must"/"have to", "make"/"compel to". These functions are instead fulfilled by periphrastic constructions involving a verb and various prepositional phrases.\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Initial <a href="/wiki/Consonant_mutation" title="Consonant mutation">consonant mutation</a></i>:  The first sound of a Cornish word may change according to grammatical context. As in Breton, there are four types of mutation in Cornish (compared to three in <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, two in <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a>, and one in <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>).  These are known as <i>soft</i> (<i>b</i> &gt; <i>v</i>, etc.), <i>hard</i> (<i>b</i> &gt; <i>p</i>), <i>aspirate</i> (<i>b</i> unchanged, <i>t</i> &gt; <i>th</i>) and <i>mixed</i> (<i>b</i> &gt; <i>f</i>).</li></ul>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">\n<caption>Consonant mutation in Cornish\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Unmutated<br />consonant\n</th>\n<th>Soft<br />mutation\n</th>\n<th>Aspirate<br />mutation\n</th>\n<th>Hard<br />mutation\n</th>\n<th>Mixed<br />mutation\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>p</td>\n<td>b</td>\n<td>f</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>-\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>t</td>\n<td>d</td>\n<td>th</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>-\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>k</td>\n<td>g</td>\n<td>h</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>-\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>b</td>\n<td>v</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>p</td>\n<td>f\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>d</td>\n<td>dh</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>t</td>\n<td>t\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>g<sup>1</sup></td>\n<td><i>disappears</i></td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>k</td>\n<td>h\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>g<sup>2</sup></td>\n<td>w</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>k</td>\n<td>hw\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>gw</td>\n<td>w</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>kw</td>\n<td>hw\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>m</td>\n<td>v</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>f\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>ch</td>\n<td>j</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>-</td>\n<td>-\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="5"><sup>1</sup> Before unrounded vowels (i, y, e, a), l, and r + unrounded vowel.<br /><sup>2</sup> Before rounded vowels (o, u), and r + rounded vowel.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Inflected_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Inflected language">inflected</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">conjugated</a>) <a href="/wiki/Preposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Preposition">prepositions</a></i>:  A preposition combines with a personal pronoun to give a separate word form.  For example, <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">gans</i></i> (with, by) + <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">my</i></i> (me) \xe2\x86\x92 <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">genev</i></i>; <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">gans</i></i> + <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">ev</i></i> (him) \xe2\x86\x92 <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">ganso</i></i>.</li>\n<li><i>No indefinite <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">article</a>.</i> <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Porth</i></i> means "harbour" or "a harbour" (there is, however, a definite article: <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">an porth</i></i> means "the harbour").</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AnGofPlaqueBlackheath.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/AnGofPlaqueBlackheath.jpg/220px-AnGofPlaqueBlackheath.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/AnGofPlaqueBlackheath.jpg/330px-AnGofPlaqueBlackheath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/AnGofPlaqueBlackheath.jpg/440px-AnGofPlaqueBlackheath.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1564" data-file-height="1112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AnGofPlaqueBlackheath.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Commemorative plaque in Cornish and English for <a href="/wiki/Michael_An_Gof" title="Michael An Gof">Michael Joseph the Smith</a> (An Gof) mounted on the north side of <a href="/wiki/Blackheath,_London" title="Blackheath, London">Blackheath</a> common, south east London, near the south entrance to Greenwich Park</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Congress" title="Celtic Congress">Celtic Congress</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_League_(political_organisation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic League (political organisation)">Celtic League</a> are groups that advocate cooperation amongst the Celtic Nations in order to protect and promote Celtic languages and cultures, thus working in the interests of the Cornish language.\n</p><p>There have been films such as <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Hwerow_Hweg" title="Hwerow Hweg">Hwerow Hweg</a></i></i>, some televised, made entirely, or significantly, in the language. Some businesses use Cornish names.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to sociolinguist Kenneth MacKinnon, Jenner wrote "There has never been a time when there has been no person in Cornwall without a knowledge of the Cornish language."<sup id="cite_ref-Diarmuid_O_Page_222_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diarmuid_O_Page_222-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cornish has significantly and durably affected Cornwall\'s place names, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Cornish_surnames" title="Cornish surnames">Cornish surnames</a>, and knowledge of the language helps the understanding of these ancient meanings. Cornish names are adopted for children, pets, houses and boats.\n</p><p>There is Cornish literature, in which poetry is the most important genre, particularly in oral form or as song or as traditional Cornish chants historically performed in marketplaces during religious holidays and public festivals and gatherings.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="What is the source for poetry being the most important genre? (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>There are periodicals solely in the language such as the monthly <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">An Gannas</i></i>, <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">An Gowsva</i></i>, and <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">An Garrick</i></i>. <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Cornwall" title="BBC Radio Cornwall">BBC Radio Cornwall</a> has a news broadcast in Cornish, and sometimes has other programmes and features for learners and enthusiasts. Local newspapers such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Western_Morning_News" title="Western Morning News">Western Morning News</a></i> have articles in Cornish, and newspapers such as <i>The Packet</i>, <i>The West Briton</i> and <i>The Cornishman</i> have also been known to have Cornish features. There is an online radio service in Cornish called <i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Radyo an Gernewegva</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-kernewegva1_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kernewegva1-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> publishing a one-hour podcast each week, based on a magazine format. It includes music in Cornish as well as interviews and features.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Music in Cornish? (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The language has financial sponsorship from sources, including the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Commission" title="Millennium Commission">Millennium Commission</a>. A number of language organisations exist in Cornwall: <i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Agan_Tavas" title="Agan Tavas">Agan Tavas</a></i> (Our Language), the Cornish sub-group of the <a href="/wiki/European_Bureau_for_Lesser-Used_Languages" title="European Bureau for Lesser-Used Languages">European Bureau for Lesser-Used Languages</a>, <i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Gorsedh_Kernow" title="Gorsedh Kernow">Gorsedh Kernow</a></i>, <i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Kesva_an_Taves_Kernewek" title="Kesva an Taves Kernewek">Kesva an Taves Kernewek</a></i> (the Cornish Language Board) and <i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Kowethas_an_Yeth_Kernewek" title="Kowethas an Yeth Kernewek">Kowethas an Yeth Kernewek</a></i> (the Cornish Language Fellowship)]. There are ceremonies, some ancient, some modern, which use the language or are entirely in the language.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Truro_Cathedral_welcome_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Truro_Cathedral_welcome_cropped.jpg/170px-Truro_Cathedral_welcome_cropped.jpg" width="170" height="304" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Truro_Cathedral_welcome_cropped.jpg/255px-Truro_Cathedral_welcome_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Truro_Cathedral_welcome_cropped.jpg/340px-Truro_Cathedral_welcome_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="536" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Truro_Cathedral_welcome_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Welcome sign at <a href="/wiki/Truro_Cathedral" title="Truro Cathedral">Truro Cathedral</a> in several languages, including Cornish.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_events">Cultural events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cultural events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Though estimations of the number of Cornish speakers vary, the speakers of Cornish today are thought to be around five hundred. Currently, Cornish is spoken by its speakers at home, outside the home, in the workplace, and at ritual ceremonies. Cornish is also being used in the arts. Revived Cornish is constructed on historical Cornish, so that the Cornish language develops. English language has had some effect in this development. Regardless of having "no concrete purpose during the twentieth century," the number of Cornish speakers has gradually increased.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Congress" title="Celtic Congress">Celtic Congress</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_League_(political_organisation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic League (political organisation)">Celtic League</a> are groups that advocate cooperation amongst the Celtic Nations in order to protect and promote Celtic languages and cultures, thus working in the interests of the Cornish language. Cornish has significantly and durably affected Cornwall\'s place names, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Cornish_surnames" title="Cornish surnames">Cornish surnames</a>, and knowledge of the language helps the understanding of these ancient meanings. Cornish names are adopted for children, pets, houses and boats. There are periodicals solely in the language such as the monthly <i>An Gannas</i>, <i>An Gowsva</i>, and <i>An Garrick</i>. <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Cornwall" title="BBC Radio Cornwall">BBC Radio Cornwall</a> has a news broadcast in Cornish, and sometimes has other programmes and features for learners and enthusiasts. Local newspapers such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Western_Morning_News" title="Western Morning News">Western Morning News</a></i> have articles in Cornish, and newspapers such as <i>The Packet</i>, <i>The West Briton</i> and <i>The Cornishman</i> have also been known to have Cornish features. The language has financial sponsorship from sources, including the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Commission" title="Millennium Commission">Millennium Commission</a>. A number of language organisations exist in Cornwall: <a href="/wiki/Agan_Tavas" title="Agan Tavas">Agan Tavas</a> (Our Language), the Cornish sub-group of the <a href="/wiki/European_Bureau_for_Lesser-Used_Languages" title="European Bureau for Lesser-Used Languages">European Bureau for Lesser-Used Languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gorsedh_Kernow" title="Gorsedh Kernow">Gorsedh Kernow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kesva_an_Taves_Kernewek" title="Kesva an Taves Kernewek">Kesva an Taves Kernewek</a> (the Cornish Language Board) and <a href="/wiki/Kowethas_an_Yeth_Kernewek" title="Kowethas an Yeth Kernewek">Kowethas an Yeth Kernewek</a> (the Cornish Language Fellowship).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cornwall has had cultural events associated with the language, including the international <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Media_Festival" title="Celtic Media Festival">Celtic Media Festival</a>, hosted in <a href="/wiki/St_Ives,_Cornwall" title="St Ives, Cornwall">St Ives</a> in 1997.  The <a href="/wiki/Old_Cornwall_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Cornwall Society">Old Cornwall Society</a> has promoted the use of the language at events and meetings. Two examples of ceremonies that are performed in both the English and Cornish languages are <a href="/wiki/Crying_the_Neck" title="Crying the Neck">Crying the Neck</a><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and the annual mid-summer bonfires.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Study_and_teaching">Study and teaching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Study and teaching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Cornish is taught in some schools; it was previously taught at degree level at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wales" title="University of Wales">University of Wales</a>, though the only existing course in the language at University level is as part of a course in Cornish Studies at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Exeter" title="University of Exeter">University of Exeter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In March 2008, a course in the language was started as part of the Celtic Studies curriculum at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a>, Austria.\nThe <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> offers courses in Cornish through its <a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Trim_Resources_Centre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="John Trim Resources Centre (page does not exist)">John Trim Resources Centre</a>, which is part of its <a href="/w/index.php?title=Language_Centre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Language Centre (page does not exist)">Language Centre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the Department of Anglo-Saxon, Norse and Celtic (which is part of the faculty of English), also carries out research into the Cornish language.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2015 a university level course aiming to encourage and support practitioners working with young children to introduce the Cornish language into their settings was launched. The <i>Cornish Language Practice Project (Early Years)</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Qualifications_and_Credit_Framework" title="Qualifications and Credit Framework">level 4</a> course approved by <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Plymouth University">Plymouth University</a> and run at <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_College" title="Cornwall College">Cornwall College</a>. The course is not a Cornish language course, but students will be assessed on their ability to use the Cornish language constructively in their work with young children. The course will cover such topics as <i>Understanding Bilingualism</i>, <i>Creating Resources</i> and <i>Integrating Language and Play</i>, but the focus of the language provision will be on Cornish. A non-accredited specialist Cornish language course has been developed to run alongside the level 4 course for those who prefer tutor support to learn the language or develop their skills further for use with young children.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cornwall\'s first Cornish language <a href="/wiki/Day_care" class="mw-redirect" title="Day care">cr\xc3\xa8che</a>, <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Skol dy\'Sadorn Kernewek</i></i>, was established in 2010 at <a href="/wiki/Cornwall_College" title="Cornwall College">Cornwall College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camborne" title="Camborne">Camborne</a>. The nursery teaches children aged between two and five years alongside their parents to ensure the language is also spoken in the home.<sup id="cite_ref-Walesonline_creche_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walesonline_creche-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A number of dictionaries are available in the different orthographies (a dictionary in the Standard Written Form has yet to be published), including <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">An Gerlyver Meur</i></i> by <a href="/wiki/Ken_George" title="Ken George">Ken George</a>, <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Gerlyver Kernowek\xe2\x80\x93Sawsnek</i></i> by <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Williams" title="Nicholas Williams">Nicholas Williams</a> and <i>A Practical Dictionary of Modern Cornish</i> by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gendall" title="Richard Gendall">Richard Gendall</a>. Course books include the three-part <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Skeul an Yeth</i></i> series, <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Clappya Kernowek</i></i>, <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Tavas a Ragadazow</i></i> and <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Skeul an Tavas</i></i>, as well as the more recent <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Bora Brav</i></i> and <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Desky Kernowek</i></i>.\n</p><p>Classes and conversation groups for adults are available at several locations in Cornwall, as well as in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maga:_class_list_and_map_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maga:_class_list_and_map-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cornish_studies">Cornish studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cornish studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/William_Scawen" title="William Scawen">William Scawen</a> produced a manuscript on the declining Cornish language that continually evolved until he died in 1689, aged 89. He was the first person to realise the language was dying out and wrote detailed manuscripts which he started working on when he was 78. The only version that was ever published was a short first draft, but the final version, which he worked on until his death, is a few hundred pages long. At the same time a group of scholars, led by <a href="/wiki/John_Keigwin" title="John Keigwin">John Keigwin</a> (nephew of William Scawen), of Mousehole, tried to preserve and further the Cornish language. They left behind a large number of translations of parts of the Bible, proverbs and songs. This group was contacted by the Welsh linguist <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lhuyd" title="Edward Lhuyd">Edward Lhuyd</a> who came to Cornwall to study the language.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early Modern Cornish was the subject of a study published by Lhuyd in 1707, and differs from the medieval language in having a considerably simpler structure and grammar. Such differences included the wide use of certain modal affixes that, although out of use by Lhuyd\'s time, had a considerable effect on the word-order of medieval Cornish<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. The medieval language also possessed two additional tenses for expressing past events and an extended set of possessive suffixes.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Whitaker_(historian)" title="John Whitaker (historian)">John Whitaker</a>, the Manchester-born rector of <a href="/wiki/Ruan_Lanihorne" title="Ruan Lanihorne">Ruan Lanihorne</a>, studied the decline of the Cornish language. In his 1804 work <i>the Ancient Cathedral of Cornwall</i> he concluded that: "[T]he English Liturgy, was not desired by the Cornish, but forced upon them by the tyranny of England, at a time when the English language was yet unknown in Cornwall. This act of tyranny was at once gross barbarity to the Cornish people, and a death blow to the Cornish language.".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Robert Williams published the first comprehensive Cornish dictionary in 1865, the <i>Lexicon Cornu-Britannicum</i>. As a result of the discovery of additional ancient Cornish manuscripts, 2000 new words were added to the vocabulary by <a href="/wiki/Whitley_Stokes_(scholar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitley Stokes (scholar)">Whitley Stokes</a> in <i>A Cornish Glossary</i>. William C. Borlase published <i>Proverbs and Rhymes in Cornish</i> in 1866 while <i>A Glossary of Cornish Place Names</i> was produced by John Bannister in the same year. <a href="/wiki/Fred_W._P._Jago" title="Fred W. P. Jago">Frederick Jago</a> published his <i>English\xe2\x80\x93Cornish Dictionary</i> in 1882.\n</p><p>In 2002, the Cornish language gained new recognition because of the European Charter for Regional and Minority Languages. Conversely, along with government provision was the governmental basis of "New Public Management," measuring quantifiable results as means of determining effectiveness. This put enormous pressure on finding a single orthography that could be used in unison. The revival of Cornish required extensive rebuilding. The Cornish orthographies that were reconstructed may be considered versions of Cornish because they are not traditional sociolinguistic variations. In the middle-to-late twentieth century, the debate over Cornish orthographies angered more people because several language groups received public funding. This caused other groups to sense favouritism as playing a role in the debate.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A governmental policymaking structure called New Public Management (NPM) has helped the Cornish language by managing public life of the Cornish language and people. In 2007, the Cornish Language Partnership MAGA represents separate divisions of government and their purpose is to further enhance the Cornish Language Developmental Plan. MAGA established an "Ad-Hoc Group," which resulted in three orthographies being presented. The relations for the Ad-Hoc Group were to obtain consensus among the three orthographies, and then develop a "single written form." The end result was creating a new form of Cornish, which had to be natural for both new learners and skilled speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_literature" title="Cornish literature">Cornish literature</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Cornish_literature">Neo-Cornish literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Neo-Cornish literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1981, the <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Breton</a> library <i><i lang="br" title="Breton language text">Preder</i></i> edited <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Passyon agan arluth</i></i> (Passion of our lord), a 15th-century Cornish poem.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The first complete <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Cornish" title="Bible translations into Cornish">translation of the Bible into Cornish</a> translated from English, was published in 2011.  Another Bible translation project translating from original languages is underway.  The New Testament and Psalms were posted on-line on YouVersion (Bible.com) and Bibles.org in July 2014 by the Bible Society.\n</p><p>A few small publishers produce books in Cornish which are stocked in some local bookshops, as well as in Cornish branches of Waterstones and WH Smiths, although newer publications are becoming increasingly available on the Internet.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Truro" title="Truro">Truro</a> Waterstones hosts the annual "<i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Holyer an Gof</i>" literary awards, established by <i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Gorsedh_Kernow" title="Gorsedh Kernow">Gorsedh Kernow</a></i> to recognise publications relating to Cornwall or in the Cornish language.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In recent years, a number of Cornish translations of literature have been published, including <i><a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Adventures_in_Wonderland" title="Alice&#39;s Adventures in Wonderland">Alice\'s Adventures in Wonderland</a></i> (2009),<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Around_the_World_in_Eighty_Days" title="Around the World in Eighty Days">Around the World in Eighty Days</a></i> (2009),<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Treasure_Island" title="Treasure Island">Treasure Island</a></i> (2010),<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>  <i><a href="/wiki/The_Railway_Children" title="The Railway Children">The Railway Children</a></i> (2012),<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Hound_of_the_Baskervilles" class="mw-redirect" title="Hound of the Baskervilles">Hound of the Baskervilles</a></i> (2012),<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i> (2012),<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wind_in_the_Willows" title="The Wind in the Willows">The Wind in the Willows</a></i> (2013),<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Men_in_a_Boat" title="Three Men in a Boat">Three Men in a Boat</a></i> (2013),<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Adventures_in_Wonderland" title="Alice&#39;s Adventures in Wonderland">Alice in Wonderland and Through the Looking-Glass</a></i> (2014),<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Christmas_Carol" title="A Christmas Carol">A Christmas Carol</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> (which won the 2012 <i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Holyer an Gof</i> award for Cornish Language books), as well as original Cornish literature such as <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Jowal Lethesow</i></i><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> (<i>The <a href="/wiki/Lyonesse" title="Lyonesse">Lyonesse</a> Stone</i>) by Craig Weatherhill. Literature aimed at children is also available, such as <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Ple\'ma Spot?</i></i> (<i>Where\'s <a href="/wiki/Spot_the_Dog" class="mw-redirect" title="Spot the Dog">Spot</a>?</i>), <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Best Goon Br\xc3\xa8n</i></i> (<i>The Beast of Bodmin Moor</i>), three <i><a href="/wiki/Topsy_and_Tim" title="Topsy and Tim">Topsy and Tim</a></i> titles,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>, two <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tintin" title="The Adventures of Tintin">Tintin</a></i> titles and <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Briallen ha\'n Alyon</i></i> (<i>Briallen and the Alien</i>), which won the 2015 <i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Holyer an Gof</i> award for Cornish Language books for children.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In 2014 <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">An Hobys</i></i>, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Williams" title="Nicholas Williams">Nicholas Williams</a>\' translation of J.R.R. Tolkien\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hobbit" title="The Hobbit">The Hobbit</a></i> was published.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/w/index.php?title=An_Gannas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="An Gannas (page does not exist)">An Gannas</a> is a monthly magazine published entirely in the Cornish language. Members contribute articles on various subjects. The magazine is produced by <a href="/wiki/Graham_Sandercock" title="Graham Sandercock">Graham Sandercock</a> who has been its editor since 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1983 <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Cornwall" title="BBC Radio Cornwall">BBC Radio Cornwall</a> started broadcasting around two minutes of Cornish every week. In 1987, however, they gave over 15 minutes of airtime on Sunday mornings for a programme called <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Kroeder Kroghen</i></i> ("<a href="/wiki/Holdall" title="Holdall">Holdall</a>"), presented by John King, running until the early 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> It was eventually replaced with a five-minute news bulletin called <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">An Nowodhow</i></i> ("The News"). The bulletin was presented every Sunday evening for many years by <a href="/wiki/Rod_Lyon" title="Rod Lyon">Rod Lyon</a>, then Elizabeth Stewart, and currently a team presents in rotation.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pirate_FM" title="Pirate FM">Pirate FM</a> ran short bulletins on Saturday lunchtimes from 1998 to 1999. In 2006, Matthew Clarke who had presented the Pirate FM bulletin, launched a web-streamed news bulletin called <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Nowodhow an Seythen</i></i> ("Weekly News"), which in 2008 was merged into a new weekly magazine podcast <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Radyo_an_Gernewegva" title="Radyo an Gernewegva">Radyo an Gernewegva</a></i></i> (RanG).\n</p><p>Cornish television shows have included a 1982 series by <a href="/wiki/Westward_Television" title="Westward Television">Westward Television</a> each episode containing a three-minute lesson in Cornish.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_Ball,_Nicole_Muller_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Ball,_Nicole_Muller-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">An Canker-Seth</i></i>, an eight episode series produced by <a href="/wiki/Television_South_West" title="Television South West">Television South West</a> and broadcast between June and July 1984, later on <a href="/wiki/S4C" title="S4C">S4C</a> from May to July 1985, and as a schools programme in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Also by Television South West were two bilingual programmes on Cornish Culture called <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Nosweyth Lowen</i></i><sup id="cite_ref-Martin_Ball,_Nicole_Muller_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Ball,_Nicole_Muller-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\nIn 2016 Kelly\'s Ice Cream of <a href="/wiki/Bodmin" title="Bodmin">Bodmin</a> introduced a light hearted television commercial in the Cornish language and this has been repeated in 2017.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>English composer <a href="/wiki/Peter_Warlock" title="Peter Warlock">Peter Warlock</a> wrote a Christmas carol in Cornish (setting words by Henry Jenner). Cornish musician <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jory_Bennett&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jory Bennett (page does not exist)">Jory Bennett</a> has composed "Six Songs of Cornwall" for bass and piano, a Cornish song-cycle, settings of Cornish language poems by <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Williams" title="Nicholas Williams">Nicholas Williams</a> /trans. <a href="/wiki/E._G._Retallack_Hooper" title="E. G. Retallack Hooper">E. G. Retallack Hooper</a> (f.p. <a href="/wiki/Keele_University" title="Keele University">Keele University</a>, 7 May 1986).  The Cornish electronic musician <a href="/wiki/Aphex_Twin" title="Aphex Twin">Aphex Twin</a> has used Cornish names for track titles, most notably on his <i><a href="/wiki/Drukqs" title="Drukqs">DrukQs</a></i> album.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Attempts have been made to recreate, in order to preserve, some Cornish folk songs, including "<a href="/wiki/An_Awhesyth" title="An Awhesyth">An Awhesyth</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Bro_Goth_agan_Tasow" title="Bro Goth agan Tasow">Bro Goth agan Tasow</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/Delkiow_Sivy" title="Delkiow Sivy">Delkiow Sivy</a>".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 2018, the singer <a href="/wiki/Gwenno_Saunders" title="Gwenno Saunders">Gwenno Saunders</a> released an album in Cornish, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Kov" title="Le Kov">Le Kov</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gwenno_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gwenno-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Placenames_and_surnames">Placenames and surnames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Placenames and surnames">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cornish_surnames" title="Cornish surnames">Cornish surnames</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CornwallKernowMappa.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/CornwallKernowMappa.png/220px-CornwallKernowMappa.png" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/CornwallKernowMappa.png/330px-CornwallKernowMappa.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/CornwallKernowMappa.png/440px-CornwallKernowMappa.png 2x" data-file-width="952" data-file-height="698" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CornwallKernowMappa.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Placenames translated into SWF</div></div></div>\n<p>The Cornish language has influenced the toponomy of Cornwall, and has historically been used in surnames for the <a href="/wiki/Cornish_people" title="Cornish people">Cornish people</a>. Long before the agreement of the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Written_Form" title="Standard Written Form">Standard Written Form of Cornish</a> in the 21st century, Late Cornish orthography in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern">Early Modern</a> period usually followed Welsh to English transliteration so phonetically rendering C for K, I for Y, U for W, and Z for S, caused place names such as Porthcurno and Penzance to be adopted into English instead of their Standard Written Form <i>Porth Kernow</i> and <i>Pen Sans</i>. Likewise, words such as Enys ("island") can be found spelled as "Ince" as at <a href="/wiki/Ince_Castle" title="Ince Castle">Ince Castle</a>. These apparent mistranslations can however reveal an insight into how names and places were actually pronounced, explaining, for example, how anglicised <a href="/wiki/Launceston,_Cornwall" title="Launceston, Cornwall">Launceston</a> is still pronounced "Lann-zan" from Cornish "Lann Stefan" with emphasis on the first element.\n</p><p>The following tables present some examples of Cornish placenames and surnames, and their anglicised versions:\n</p>\n<div>\n\n&#32;\n<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"><tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 50%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>English (anglicised) language text">Tewynblustri</i></td>\n<td>Dunes [of] Pleustri\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Penzance" title="Penzance">Penzance</a></td>\n<td><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cornish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Samples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>From the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>:\n</p>\n<table border="0" style="width:100%; text-align:left; margin-bottom: 3em;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Cornish\n</th>\n<th>Translation\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Genys frank ha par yw oll tus an bys</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>All human beings are born free and\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">yn aga dynita hag yn aga gwiryow.</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>equal in dignity and rights. They are\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Enduys yns gans reson ha kowses</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>endowed with reason and conscience\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">hag y tal dhedha omdhon an eyl orth</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>and should act towards one another\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">y gila yn spyrys a vrederedh.</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>in a spirit of brotherhood.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>From <i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text"><a href="/wiki/Bro_Goth_agan_Tasow" title="Bro Goth agan Tasow">Bro Goth agan Tasow</a></i></i>, the Cornish anthem:\n</p>\n<table border="0" style="width:100%; text-align:left;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Cornish\n</th>\n<th>Translation\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Bro goth agan tasow, dha fleghes a\'th kar,</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Old land of our fathers, your children love you,\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Gwlas ker an howlsedhes, pan vro yw dha bar?</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Dear country of the west, what land is your equal?\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">War oll an norvys \'th on ni skollys a-les,</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Over all the world, we are spread far and wide,\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Mes agan kerensa yw dhis.</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>But our love is for you.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Kernow, Kernow y keryn Kernow;</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>Cornwall, Cornwall, we love Cornwall;\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">An mor hedre vo yn fos dhis a-dro</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>For as long as the sea is a wall around you\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><i lang="kw" title="Cornish language text">Th on onan hag oll rag Kernow!</i></i>\n</td>\n<td>We are one and all for Cornwall!\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span
